Qisas — Retaliation
intermediate
8 min
Validity of a Slave's Confession to Intentional Murder
Debate between Hanafi and Zufar on slave's confession for qisas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on a slave's confession to intentional murder and explain why it differs from a slave's confession of a financial debt, using the concepts of tuhmah and asl al-hurriyah.

Core Idea
A slave's life is not property; his confession to murder is accepted because it is against his own interest, eliminating collusion.

This passage addresses a delicate question: when a slave confesses to intentional murder, does his confession count for qisas? Imam Zufar argued that it cannot, likening it to a financial confession. But the Hanafi majority saw a crucial difference—one that goes to the heart of how Islamic law views human life versus property. Let's trace their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling and the opponent
Identify the act: a slave confesses to intentional murder. Note the Hanafi ruling (valid, qisas obligatory) and the opponent (Zufar).
2
Understand Zufar's qiyas
Zufar draws an analogy to a slave's confession of debt. Debts nullify the master's property rights, so such confessions are invalid. Similarly, murder confession nullifies the master's right to the slave's life, so it should also be invalid.
This is the opposing dalil.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response: tuhmah and asl al-hurriyah
The Hanafi response rejects the analogy on two grounds: (1) In murder confession, the slave is not mutttaham (accused of collusion) because the confession harms himself, unlike financial confession where collusion is possible. (2) In blood matters, the slave is treated as free (asl al-hurriyah) because of his humanity (adamiyyah); qisas is about blood, not property.
This distinguishes the two cases.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical difference? Under Zufar, the master could block qisas. Under Hanafi, the slave is executed even if the master objects.
5
Determine the tarjih and authority
The text states 'our madhhab is valid'. This is the mufta bihi position from zahir al-riwayah. The authority weight is high.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غلام قتل عمد کا اقرار کرے آیا اس کا اقرار معتبر ہے یا نہیں
قال واذا اقر العبد بقتل العمد لزمه القود وقال زفر لا يصح اقراره لانه يلا قى حق المولى بالابطال فصار كما اذا اقر بالمال ولنا انه غير متهم فيه لانه مضربه فيقبل ولان العبد مبقى على اصل الحرية في حق الدم عملا بالادمية حتى لا يصح اقرار المولى عليه بالحدود والقصاص وبطلان حق المولى بطريق الضمن فلا يبالی به
ترجمہ قدوری نے فرمایا اور جب کہ غلام نے قتل عمد کا اقرار کیا تو اس پر قصاص لازم ہو گا اور زفر نے فرمایا کہ اس کا اقرار صحیح نہ ہو گا اس لئے کہ اس کا اقرار ملاقی ہوا ہے حق مولیٰ سے باطل کرنے کے ساتھ تو ایسا ہو گیا جب کہ غلام نے مال کا اقرار کیا ہو اور ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ غلام اس میں متہم نہیں ہے۔ اس لئے کہ اقرار غلام کیلئے مضر ہے تو اس کا اقرار قبول کر لیا جائے گا اور اس لئے کہ غلام خون کے حق میں اصل حریت پر باقی ہے آدمیت پر عمل کرتے ہوئے یہاں تک کہ علائم آقا کا حدود و قصاص کا اقرار صیح نہ ہوگا اور آقا کے حق کا بطلان ضمناً ہے تو اس کی پر اوہ نہیں کی جائے گی۔
تشریح غلام قتل عمد کا اقرار کرتا ہے تو اس کا اقرار معتبر ہوگا یا نہیں اس میں امام زفر کا قول یہ ہے کہ اگر اس کا اقرار معتبر ہو جائے تو غلام کو قصاص میں قتل کر دیا جائے گا اور مولیٰ کا حق باطل ہو جائے گا حالانکہ غلام جب ایسا اقرار کرے جس میں آقا کا حق باطل ہوتا ہو تو وہاں اس کا اقرار معتبر نہیں ہوتا۔ مثلاً غلام نے اقرار کیا کہ فلاں کے میرے ذمہ اتنے روپے ہیں تو یہ اقرار معتبر نہ ہو گا اسی طرح قتل عمد کا اقرار بھی درست نہ ہوگا۔
ہمارا مذ ہب یہ ہے کہ اقرار سیح ہے اور غلام سے قصاص لیا جائے گا۔ رہا آقا کے حق کا بطلان تو وہ ضمنی چیز ہے مقصود اصلی نہیں ہے۔ کیونکہ اس اقرار میں غلام پر کوئی تہمت نہیں اور مال کے اقرار میں تہمت ہے کیونکہ آقا سے زیادہ نقصان تو خود غلام کا ہے کہ اس کی جان جا رہی ہے لہذا اس کا اقرار مقبول ہو گا۔
پھر اگر آقا اپنے غلام پر حدود یا قصاص کا اقرار کرے تو اس کا اقرار صحیح نہ ہوگا اور مولی کے اقرار کی وجہ سے غلام پر حد یا قصاص جاری نہ ہو گا تو اس مسئلہ سے یہ بات معلوم ہوگئی کہ غلام اگر چہ غلام اور مملوک ہے لیکن اس کی آدمیت اور انسانیت کا لحاظ کرتے ہوئے یہ قاعدہ مقرر ہوا کہ غلام اپنے خون کے اندر حریت کی صفت رکھتا ہے اور قصاص کا مقصد اصلی خون بہانا ہے نہ کہ مال بلکہ مال کا معدوم ہو نانمنی چیز ہے لہذا یہاں غلام کا اقرار اس چیز کے بارے میں معتبر ہوا ہے۔ جو چیز کہ حریت کی صفت پر برقرار ہے۔
A slave confesses to intentional murder – is his confession valid or not? He (al-Quduri) said: When a slave confesses to intentional murder, retribution becomes binding on him. Zufar said: His confession is not valid because it confronts the master's right with nullification, so it becomes like when he confesses to property. And our proof: He is not accused in this, because it is harmful to him, so his confession is accepted. And because the slave is retained on original freedom regarding blood, acting on humanity, so that the master's confession of hudud and qisas is not valid, and the nullification of the master's right is incidental, so it is not considered. Translation: Al-Quduri said: When a slave confesses to intentional murder, retribution becomes binding on him. Zufar said: His confession is not valid, because his confession meets the right of the master with nullification, so it becomes like when a slave confesses to property. And our proof: The slave is not accused in this, because the confession is harmful to the slave, so his confession is accepted. And because the slave remains on original freedom in the right of blood, acting on humanity, so much so that the master's confession of hudud and qisas is not valid, and the nullification of the master's right is incidental, so it is not considered. Explanation: The slave confesses to intentional murder – will his confession be valid or not? In this, Imam Zufar's opinion is: If his confession is deemed valid, the slave will be killed in retribution, and the master's right will be nullified, whereas when a slave makes a confession that nullifies the master's right, his confession is not valid there. For example, if a slave confesses that someone has so many rupees due from me, this confession is not valid; similarly, the confession of intentional murder would also not be valid. Our madhhab is that the confession is valid and retribution will be taken from the slave. As for the nullification of the master's right, it is an incidental matter, not the primary objective. Because in this confession there is no accusation against the slave, whereas in the confession of property there is an accusation, because the greater loss is to the slave himself – his life is taken. Therefore, his confession is accepted. Then, if the master confesses hudud or qisas against his own slave, his confession is not valid, and the limit or retribution will not be executed on the slave due to the master's confession. So from this issue it becomes known that although the slave is a slave and owned, but considering his humanity and human nature, this principle is established: the slave possesses the attribute of freedom regarding his own blood, and the primary objective of qisas is to shed blood, not property; rather, the loss of property is an insignificant thing. Therefore, the slave's confession is valid regarding that thing which remains on the attribute of freedom.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage addresses an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of a slave's confession to intentional murder (qatl amd). The text opens by presenting the classical Hanafi position (based on al-Quduri's matn) that the confession is accepted and qisas becomes obligatory. It then records the dissenting view of Imam Zufar (Zufar ibn al-Hudhayl), who draws an analogy to a slave's confession of a financial debt: since such a confession would nullify the master's property rights, it is invalid; similarly, confessing to murder nullifies the master's right to the slave's life (as property) through qisas, so it should also be invalid. The Hanafi response refutes this analogy on two grounds. First, the slave is not tainted (mutttaham) because the confession is against his own interest (mudirr) – he is risking his life, whereas in financial confession, he might be colluding with others to harm the master. Second, in matters of blood and life, the slave is retained on his original freedom (asl al-hurriyah) due to his humanity (adamiyyah). This principle is evident from the fact that the master's own confession of hudud or qisas against his slave is not accepted. Thus, the nullification of the master's right is incidental (bil-tariq al-dhimni) and not the primary effect. The commentary further clarifies that the primary purpose of qisas is retributive justice, not property compensation, so the property aspect is insignificant. Therefore, the slave's confession is valid, and he will face qisas.

Key Terms
اقرار (iqrar): A voluntary admission or confession of a right or claim against oneself.
تہمت (tuhmah): Accusation or suspicion of collusion or falsehood; in the context of a slave's confession, it refers to the possibility that the slave is colluding with a third party to harm the master.
مولیٰ (mawla): The master or owner of a slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is a slave's confession of intentional murder accepted despite potentially nullifying the master's right?
A) Because the slave is not accused (mutttaham) since the confession harms himself.
B) Because the master's consent is not required.
C) Because the slave is considered free in all matters.
D) Because the qisas can be commuted to diyah.

Key Takeaway
A slave's confession to intentional murder is valid and triggers qisas because it is against his own interest (no tuhmah) and blood matters are governed by original freedom, not property.

Liability for Multiple Deaths from a Single Intentional Act
Ruling on two killings from one intentional shot

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when one intentional act causes two deaths and explain the principle that makes the act legally multiple.

Core Idea
One act, two deaths—the act is legally multiple because its effects are multiple, yielding qisas for the first and diyah for the second.

This lesson examines a scenario where a single intentional shot kills two persons. The Hanafi school distinguishes between the intended victim and the unintended second victim, applying different penalties: qisas for the first, diyah on the 'aqilah for the second. The key is the legal principle that an act multiplies by multiplicity of effect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the two killings
Read the passage to understand the factual situation: a person intentionally shoots an arrow (or bullet) at one person; the projectile passes through and kills a second person. Both die. Recognize that there are two distinct deaths with different intentions.
The first death is intended (amd), the second is unintended (khata).
2
State the ruling for each victim
From the passage, derive the penalty: for the first victim, qisas (retribution) is wajib; for the second victim, diyah (blood money) is wajib on the killer's 'aqilah (tribal group). Note that the killer does not face qisas for the second death.
The ruling is clear: 'عليه القصاص للأول والدية للثاني على عاقلته'.
3
Trace the proof: the principle and the analogy
The passage cites the principle 'الفعل يتعدد بتعدد الأثر' (the act becomes multiple by multiplicity of effect) and an analogy to shooting at game but hitting a human. Understand how the proof works: the single physical act is treated as two legal acts because the effects (deaths) are distinct in terms of intention.
The analogy shows that intention is specific to the primary target, making the secondary death accidental.
4
Resolve the objection: how one act yields two penalties
The passage addresses the objection that one act cannot have two different penalties. The answer: when the effect is multiple, the act is legally multiple. Therefore, the killer faces two separate liabilities corresponding to the two deaths.
This is a key point for understanding Hanafi causation theory in criminal law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عمداً ایک شخص کو تیر مارادوہ دوسرے کو بھی جالگا اور دونوں فوت ہو گئے تو کیا حکم ہے؟
ومن رمى رجلا عمدا فنفذ السهم منه الى اخر فما تافعليه القصاص للاول والدية للثاني على عاقلته لان الأول عمد والثانى احد نوعى الخطاء كانه رمى الى صيد فاصاب ادمياً والفعل يتعدد بتعدد الاثر.
ترجمہ اور جس نے کسی شخص کو تیر مار اعد اپس وہ تیر اس سے دوسرے کو جالگا پس وہ دونوں مر گئے تو اس پر اول کے لئے قصاص اور ثانی کے لئے اس کی برادری پر دیت واجب ہوگی۔ اس لئے کہ اول عمد ہے اور ثانی خطاء کی دو قسموں میں سے ایک ہے گویا کہ اس نے شکار کی طرف تیر پھینکا پس وہ کسی آدمی کو لگا اور فعل متعدد ہو جاتا ہے اثر کے متعدد ہونے سے۔
تشریح زید نے ایک شخص کو گولی ماری اس کو وہ گولی لگی اور اس کے بدن سے پار ہو کر دوسرے شخص کو بھی گیا اور اول بھی۔ اب کیا حکم ہے؟ تو فرمایا کہ اول قتل تو ظاہر ہے کہ قتل عمد ہے اور قتل عمد کی سزا قصاص ہے اور ثانی قتل خطاء ہے خواہ اس کو خطا : فی القصد کہا جائے یا خطاء فی الفعل بہر حال قتل خطاء ہے اور قتل خطاء میں دیت واجب ہوتی ہے لہذا اقتل اول کی وجہ سے قصاص واجب ہوگا اور قتل ثانی کی وجہ سے اس کی برادری پر دیت واجب ہوگی۔
سوال یہ تو عجیب بات ہے کہ فعل ایک اور سز ا متعدد؟
جوار جب اثر میں تعدد ہو جائے تو فعل کو بھی متعدد شمار کر لیا جاتا ہے۔ لہذا فعل متعدد ہو گیا۔
If someone intentionally shoots an arrow at a person, and the arrow passes through him and hits another, and both die, what is the ruling? And he (al-Quduri) said: Whoever intentionally shoots an arrow at a man, and the arrow penetrates him and hits another, and both die, then upon him is qisas for the first and diyah on his 'aqilah (tribal group) for the second. Because the first is intentional (amd) and the second is one of the two types of error (khata). It is as if he shot at game and hit a human. The act becomes multiple by the multiplicity of effect. Translation: And whoever intentionally shoots an arrow at a person, then that arrow goes through him and hits another, so both die, then upon him is qisas for the first, and for the second, diyah on his 'aqilah. Because the first is intentional and the second is one of the two types of error, as if he threw an arrow at game and it hit a human. The act becomes multiple by the multiplicity of effect. Explanation: Zaid shot a bullet at a person; the bullet hit him and pierced through his body and hit another person, and the first also died. Now what is the ruling? He (the author) said: The first killing is obviously intentional murder (qatl amd), and the penalty for intentional murder is qisas. The second killing is erroneous (qatl khata), whether it is called error in intention or error in action; in any case it is erroneous killing, and for erroneous killing, diyah is obligatory. Therefore, due to the first killing, qisas is obligatory, and due to the second killing, diyah on his 'aqilah is obligatory. Question: This is strange – the act is one, but the penalties are multiple? Answer: When the effect becomes multiple, the act is also considered multiple. Therefore, the act becomes multiple.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling with proof from al-Quduri's matn, expanded in the commentary. The scenario: A person intentionally shoots an arrow (or bullet) at one person; the projectile passes through the first victim and strikes a second, killing both. The ruling: For the first victim, qisas (retribution) is wajib because the killing is intentional (amd). For the second victim, diyah (blood money) is wajib on the killer's 'aqilah (tribal group) because the killing is erroneous (khata) – even though the act was intentional, the effect on the second person was unintended, falling under one of the two types of khata (error in action or error in intention). The proof is the legal principle: 'The act becomes multiple by the multiplicity of effect' (الفعل يتعدد بتعدد الأثر). The commentary clarifies by analogy: it is as if one intentionally shot at game but hit a human – the intention was directed at game, but the actual target was human, making it khata. The text then addresses a potential objection: how can one act give rise to two different penalties? The answer: when an act has multiple effects, it is legally treated as multiple acts. Hence, the killer faces qisas for the first and diyah for the second.

Key Terms
اثر (athar): Effect or consequence of an act; here, the multiplicity of killing outcomes (two deaths) makes the act legally multiple.
خطاء (khata): Error or mistake; in criminal law, it refers to killing without intent to kill, either by accident or mistake of target.
عاقلة ('aqilah): Tribe or group of paternal relatives who bear the diyah for erroneous killings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario of an arrow passing through and killing two persons, what is the penalty for the first victim?
A) Diyah on the 'aqilah
B) Qisas (retribution)
C) No penalty because it was accidental
D) Half diyah

Key Takeaway
One act causing multiple deaths is legally multiple; the killer is liable for qisas for the intended and diyah for the unintended.

Qisas — Retaliation

Separate Liability for Cutting Hand and Killing
Enumeration of four scenarios with a uniform ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule: when a person cuts off a hand and then kills the same victim, he is liable for both acts separately, regardless of order, intent, or healing status.

Core Idea
Two separate acts require two separate compensations—even if done by the same person to the same victim.

This passage from al-Quduri presents a fasl (chapter) on a specific combination of injuries: a hand cutting followed by a killing. The key question is whether the two acts merge into one liability or remain distinct. The Hanafi ruling is clear: the perpetrator is taken for both matters (yakhudhu bil-amrayn jamiʿan) in all four possible combinations. Understanding this principle is essential for calculating diyat and qisas correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four scenarios
List the four combinations of hand cutting (ʿamd or khata) and subsequent killing (ʿamd or khata), with the hand either healing or not before death.
The text gives all four: (1) khata cut + ʿamd kill before healing; (2) ʿamd cut + khata kill before healing; (3) khata cut + healing + khata kill; (4) ʿamd cut + healing + ʿamd kill.
2
Extract the common ruling
In each scenario, the text says 'he is taken for both matters' (fa innahu yuʿkhadh bi-l-amrayn jamiʿan). This means separate liability for the hand injury and for the killing.
The ruling applies regardless of intent (ʿamd/khata) and regardless of whether the hand healed before death.
3
Understand the distinction from a single act
The commentary explains that unlike the previous fasl (where a single arrow had multiple effects), here there are two distinct acts, each with its own liability.
This principle prevents merging of compensations even if the acts are sequential and by the same perpetrator.
4
Apply to a concrete example
Example: If A cuts B's hand intentionally (ʿamd) and B's hand heals, then A intentionally kills B – A must pay diyat for the hand (half diyat of a person) and face qisas or diyat for the killing.
The hand injury is not absorbed into the death penalty.

Source Text

وهم فصل قال ومن قطع يد رجل خطاء ثم قتله عمدا قبل ان تبرأ يده او قطع يده عمدا ثم قتله خطا او قطع يده خطأ فبرأت يده ثم قتله خطاء او قطع يده عمدًا فبرأت ثم قتله عمدا فانه يؤخذ بالامرين جميعًا
ترجمہ ی فصل ہے قدوری نے فرمایا اور جس نے کسی شخص کا ہاتھ خطاء کا ٹا پھر اس کو عدا اس کا ہاتھ اچھا ہونے سے پہلے قتل کر دیا اس کا ہاتھ عد اقطع کیا، پھر اس کو خطا قتل کیا یا اس کے ہاتھ کو خطاء کاٹا پس اس کا ہاتھ اچھا ہو گیا پھر اس کو خطاء قتل کیا یا اس کا ہاتھ عمدا کا ناپس وہ اچھا ہو گیا پھر اس کو عمد اقتل کیا تو وہ دونوں چیزوں کے بارے میں ماخوذ ہوگا۔
تشریح پہلی فصل میں فعل واحد کا ذکر تھا اس فصل میں دو فعلوں کا ذکر ہے مذکور متن میں مصنف نے چار صورتیں بیان کی ہیں جس میں فاعل کا فعل متعدد ہے اور دونوں فعلوں کا الگ الگ ضمان لیا جائے گا۔ (1) ہاتھ خطا کا ٹا پھر عمد اقتل کیا اور درمیان میں ہاتھ ٹھیک نہ ہوا ہو ۔ (۲) ہاتھ عمداً کاٹا اور درمیان میں برکت نہ ہوئی ہو پھر خطا قتل کیا ہو۔ (۳) خطا ر ہا تھ کا ٹا درمیان میں ٹھیک ہو گیا پھر خطا قتل کر دیا ہو۔
The ruling for four scenarios in which the doer's act is multiple. And they are: He (al-Quduri) said: Whoever cuts off a man's hand erroneously (khata) then kills him intentionally (amd) before his hand heals, or cuts off his hand intentionally then kills him erroneously, or cuts off his hand erroneously and the hand heals then kills him erroneously, or cuts off his hand intentionally and it heals then kills him intentionally – he will be taken for both matters. Translation: This is the chapter. Al-Quduri said: And whoever cuts off a person's hand erroneously, then kills him intentionally before his hand heals; or cuts off his hand intentionally, then kills him erroneously; or cuts off his hand erroneously, then his hand heals, then kills him erroneously; or cuts off his hand intentionally, then it heals, then kills him intentionally – then he will be taken for both matters. Explanation: In the first chapter (previous section) the discussion was of a single act. In this chapter, there is mention of two acts. In the mentioned text, the author has stated four scenarios in which the doer's act is multiple, and separate compensation (daman) will be taken for both acts. (1) He cut the hand erroneously, then killed intentionally, and the hand did not heal in between. (2) He cut the hand intentionally, and the hand did not heal in between, then killed erroneously. (3) He cut the hand erroneously, it healed in between, then killed erroneously...
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a new fasl (chapter) from al-Quduri's matn, dealing with scenarios where the perpetrator commits two distinct acts: cutting a hand and then killing the same victim. The text lists four possible combinations of the hand cutting (either intentional (amd) or erroneous (khata)) and the subsequent killing (either amd or khata), with the condition of whether the hand had healed (bar'at) before the killing. In all four scenarios, the perpetrator is liable for both acts – i.e., he must pay damages (daman) for the hand and also face the penalty for the killing. The commentary notes that this contrasts with the previous fasl (arrow scenario) where a single act had multiple effects; here there are two separate acts, each with its own liability. The text provides three scenarios explicitly in the provided excerpt: (1) erroneous hand cutting followed by intentional killing before healing; (2) intentional hand cutting followed by erroneous killing before healing; (3) erroneous hand cutting, healing, then erroneous killing. The fourth scenario (intentional hand cutting, healing, then intentional killing) is mentioned in the Arabic but the commentary is cut off.

Key Terms
ید (yad): Hand; in this context, the body part that is cut off, causing injury.
برءت (bar'at): Healing or recovery of the wound; if the hand heals before the killing, it indicates the injury was separate from the death.
ضمان (daman): Liability for compensation; here it refers to both diyah for the hand and qisas or diyah for the killing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is true about the four scenarios?
A) The liability depends on the order of the acts.
B) The liability depends on whether the hand healed.
C) The perpetrator is liable for both acts in all four scenarios.
D) only the intentional killing is punished if the hand was cut intentionally.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, when a person commits two separate physical acts against the same victim – cutting a hand and then killing – the liabilities for each remain distinct and are enforced separately.

Qisas — Retaliation
intermediate

Combining Intentional Hand-Cutting and Killing: The Hanafi Intra-Madhhab Dispute
General rule on combining injuries plus application to intentional hand-cut then kill

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Imam Abu Hanifa's and the Sahibayn's positions on combining intentional hand-cutting and killing before healing, explain the role of equivalence (musāwāt) in qisas, and evaluate the practical consequence (thamarah) of the dispute.

Core Idea
Intentional acts of different qisas types cannot be combined because equivalence (musāwāt) is required.

This passage from Al-Hidayah presents a general principle on combining multiple injuries (tadakhul) and then applies it to a specific intra-Hanafi dispute: if someone intentionally cuts off another's hand and then kills him before the wound heals, should both qisas be enforced or only the greater (death)? Imam Abu Hanifa offers the imam an option; the two companions (Sahibayn) insist on combination, dropping the hand amputation. Understanding the reasoning reveals how the Hanafi school treats the requirement of exact equivalence in retributive justice.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule on combining injuries
The passage opens with the principle that combining (jam') multiple injuries into a single liability is obligatory when possible, to complete the first act. If combination is impossible, each injury is treated separately.
This rule applies to both intentional and accidental acts, but its application differs.
2
Identify the disputed scenario
The specific case is: someone intentionally cuts off another's hand, then intentionally kills him before the hand wound heals. The question is whether the two acts are combined (tadakhul) or treated separately.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and proof
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that the acts are combined, so only execution is carried out; the hand amputation is dropped. Their proof: both acts are intentional (similar) and no healing intervened, so combination is possible by the general principle.
4
State Abu Hanifa's position and proof
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the imam has an option: either amputate the hand first then execute, or execute directly. The acts are not combined because the two types of qisas require exact equivalence (hand for hand, life for life) — they are fundamentally different. Additionally, the cutting severs the causal continuity, making the killing an independent act.
Abu Hanifa emphasizes that similarity of intention does not override the need for equivalence in the specific act.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Under Abu Hanifa, the criminal may suffer both amputation and execution; under the Sahibayn, only execution is carried out, and the hand amputation is waived.
The passage does not specify which view is mufta bihi; both are transmitted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والاصل فيه ان الجمع بين الجراحات واجب ما امكن تتميما للاول لان القتل في الاعم يقع بضربات متعاقبة وفى اعتبار كل ضربة بنفسها بعض الحرج الا ان لا يمكن الجمع فيعطى كل واحد حكم نفسه وقد تعذر الجمع في هذه الفصول فى الاولين لاختلاف حكم الفعلين وفى الاخرين لتخلل البرأة وهو قاطع للسواية حتى لو لم يتخلل وقد تجانسابان كانا خطأين يجمع بالاجماع لامكان الجمع واكتفى بدية واحدة. وان كان قطع يده عمداً ثم قتله عمداً قبل ان تبرأ يده فان شاء الامام قال اقطعوه ثم اقتلوه وان شاء قال اقتلوه وهذا عند ابي حنيفة وقالا يقتل ولا تقطع يده لان الجمع ممكن لتجانس الفعلين وعدم تخلل البرأ فيجمع بينهما. وله ان الجمع متعذر اما للاختلاف بين الفعلين هذين لان الموجب القود وهو يعتمد المساواة في الفعل وذالك بان يكون القتل بالقتل والقطع بالقطع وهو معذر او لان الحز يقطع اضافة السراية الى القطع حتى لوصدر امن شخصين يجب القود على الحاز قصار كنخلل البر بخلاف ما اذا قطع اوسرى ولان الفعل واحد و بخلاف ما اذا كانا خطأين لان الموجب الدية وهى بدل النفس من غير اعتبار المساواة
The principle in this is that combining injuries is obligatory as much as possible to complete the first, because killing generally occurs by successive blows, and considering each blow individually involves some hardship. But if combination is not possible, each is given its own ruling. And combination in these cases is not possible: in the first two due to difference in the rulings of the two acts, and in the last two due to the intervening healing, which cuts off the continuity. If there is no intervening healing and both acts are similar, e.g., both inadvertent, they are combined by consensus because combination is possible, and one blood money suffices. And if he cut off his hand intentionally, then killed him intentionally before his hand healed, then if the imam wishes he may say: cut off his hand then kill him, and if he wishes he may say: kill him. This is according to Abu Hanifa. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: he is killed and his hand is not cut off, because combination is possible due to the similarity of the two acts and the absence of intervening healing, so they are combined. And [Abu Hanifa's] proof: combination is impossible either due to the difference between these two acts (because each mandates retaliation, which requires equivalence in the act: killing for killing and cutting for cutting, and that is impossible), or because the cutting severs the attribution of continuity to the cut, so that even if it were from two persons, retaliation would be required from the cutter, like the intervening healing, unlike if the cut had not healed, and because the act is one, unlike if both were inadvertent because the obligation is blood money, which is a substitute for the person without consideration of equivalence.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage presents a general legal rule (qā'ida kulliyya) in the Hanafi school regarding multiple injuries inflicted on the same person. The rule: combine (jam') the injuries into a single liability whenever possible, to complete the earlier act; if combination is impossible, each injury is treated separately. The text then applies this to a specific scenario: someone intentionally cuts off another's hand and then intentionally kills him before the wound heals. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the imam/guardian has an option: either have the hand amputated as qisas and then execute the killer, or just execute. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that only execution is carried out; the hand amputation is dropped because the two acts are similar (both intentional) and no healing intervened, so they are combined. Abu Hanifa argues that combination is impossible because the two acts are fundamentally different: qisas for the hand requires equivalence in the specific organ, while qisas for killing requires equivalence in life. Additionally, the cutting severs the continuity (sarāya) of the earlier injury, preventing it from contributing to the death. He contrasts this with two inadvertent acts, which are combined because the obligation is blood money (diyah), not retribution requiring exact equivalence.

Key Terms
تداخل (tadakhul): The principle of combining multiple liabilities into a single liability when acts are similar and no healing intervenes.
قصاص (qisas): Retributive punishment equivalent to the injury inflicted.
سراية (sarāya): The continuity of an injury leading to further harm or death; if healing intervenes, continuity is broken.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, why is combination (tadakhul) impossible when someone intentionally cuts off a hand and then intentionally kills the same person before healing?
A) Because the acts are different in type (hand vs life) requiring separate equivalence in qisas.
B) Because the victim's hand healed after the cut.
C) Because the killer repented before the killing.
D) Because the killing was accidental, not intentional.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, combining multiple injuries (tadakhul) requires similarity in the nature of the act and the ruling; for qisas, exact equivalence prevents combination of different types of harm.

Qisas — Retaliation

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Tadakhul: Intentional Cutting then Killing
Intra-Hanafi dispute on whether penalties merge when hand is cut then victim killed before wound spreads

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on tadakhul when a limb is cut and then the victim is killed intentionally, and explain the evidence of musawat and the barat analogy.

Core Idea
When a hand is cut and then the victim is killed intentionally, the penalties do not merge because each act requires its own qisas for equality.

The Hanafi school faces an internal dispute on whether penalties merge (tadakhul) when a person intentionally cuts off someone's hand and then kills them before the wound spreads. Imam Abu Hanifa argues that the penalties remain separate, while his two leading students, the Sahibayn, hold that only one penalty suffices. This lesson unpacks Abu Hanifa's reasoning, focusing on the necessity of equality (musawat) in qisas and the novel analogy of barat (interruption) that he employs to justify non-merger.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position on tadakhul in amd
State that Imam Abu Hanifa holds that no tadakhul occurs when a limb is cut intentionally and then the victim is killed intentionally before the wound spreads. Both qisas for the hand and qisas for the soul must be applied separately.
The Sahibayn disagree, advocating for merger.
2
Pinpoint the Sahibayn's opposing view
The Sahibayn argue that only qisas for the killing is due, implying that the hand penalty merges into the killing penalty.
This is the opposing position within the Hanafi school.
3
Examine Abu Hanifa's first proof: musawat (equality)
Qisas requires exact equality: a hand for a hand, a life for a life. If only one qisas is applied, the hand injury remains unpunished, violating musawat. Abu Hanifa stresses that the two acts differ in essence (limb vs. soul), so they cannot be treated as one.
Musawat is a core condition for qisas.
4
Analyze the barat (interruption) analogy
Abu Hanifa draws an analogy to a case where a third party kills after the hand cut: by consensus, the acts are independent (no tadakhul). Here, the killer's own act of killing likewise severs the causal chain of the hand wound, creating an interruption equivalent to barat, thus preventing merger.
The example of Zaid and Bakr illustrates this: Bakr's killing cuts off spread, so only Bakr is liable for qisas.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Under Abu Hanifa's view, the offender must undergo qisas for the hand (hand cut) and then qisas for the soul (execution). Under the Sahibayn's view, only execution is required. The mufta bihi position follows Abu Hanifa.
This difference has direct implications for sentencing in intentional bodily harm followed by homicide.

للسراية فيجتمع ضمة الكل وضمان الجزء في حالة واحدة ولا يجتمعان اما القطع والقتل قصاصاً يـجـتـمـعـان ترجمہ اور اس لئے ہاتھ کا جرمانہ فعل کے اثر کے استحکام کے وقت ہوتا ہے اور استحکام اس گردن کاٹنے کی وجہ سے ہو گیا جو سرایت کو ختم کر دینے والی ہے تو کل کا ضمان اور جز کا ضمان ایک حالت میں جمع ہو جاتا ہے حالانکہ یہ دونوں جمع نہیں ہوا کرتے بہر حال قطع وقتل بطور قصاص کے یہ دونوں جمع ہو جاتے ہیں۔ تشریح اگر ہاتھ کاٹنا اور قتل کرنا دونوں خطاء ہوں تو صرف دیت پر اکتفاء کیوں ہوتا ہے یہ اس کی دوسری دلیل ہے۔ فرماتے ہیں کہ اگر ہاتھ کاٹنے کی وجہ سے زخم سرایت کر کے موت تک نہ پہنچے تو ہاتھ کاٹنے کی سزا ہاتھ کی دیت ہے یعنی پانچ ہزار درہم جو پوری دیت نفس کا نصف ہے اور اگر سرایت کر کے موت تک پہنچ جائے تو پھر پوری دیت واجب ہوتی ہے۔ بہر حال ہاتھ کا ارش ( دیت) تب واجب ہو گا جب کہ فعل کا اثر مستحکم ہو جائے یعنی صرف ہاتھ کٹنے تک رہے اور آگے نہ بڑھے اور یہاں صورت مذکورہ میں استحکام کا علم ہو گا گردن کاٹنے کے بعد کیونکہ اب زخم سرایت کرنے کا مبل نہیں رہا تو اب آپ ہاتھ کی دیت کو واجب کریں گے تو مطلب یہ ہوا کہ گردن کاٹنے کی وجہ سے آپ نے ہاتھ کی دیت واجب کی اور اسی گردن کاٹنے کی وجہ سے ہی آپ نے پوری دیت واجب کی اور پوری دیت میں ہاتھ کی دیت موجود ہے کیونکہ کل جز کو شامل ہوا کرتا ہے جیسے دس ایک کو بھی شامل ہے تو خلاصہ یہ ہوا کہ آپ نے ہاتھ کی دیت دو مرتبہ حاصل کر لی پہلے انفرادا اور پھر پوری دیت کے ساتھ ملا کر اسی کو مصنف نے عمان کل اور ضمان جز سے تعبیر کیا ہے۔ ولا يجتمعان ... حالانکہ یہ جائز نہیں کہ ایک عضو کی دیت ڈبل ڈبل وصول کی جائے لہذا ہم کو مجبوراً کہنا پڑا کہ یہاں صرف ایک نئی دیت واجب ہوگی اور تداخل ہو جائے گا اس لئے خطاء کی صورت میں ہم مجبورا تداخل کے قائل ہو گئے ہیں۔ اور عمداً کی صورت میں
The Book of Jinayat. Equality: That killing is in exchange for killing and cutting of hand is in exchange for cutting of hand, and this equality is when there is only qisas and cutting of hand is abandoned? It is impossible, or because cutting the neck prevents the spread of the cut hand, such that if both (intentional cutting and killing) are done by two persons, qisas is obligatory on the one who cut the neck, so it becomes as if barat (interruption) occurred between them, unlike the case when he cut the hand and it spread (then qisas for killing only) because it is one act. And unlike when both are accidental, because accidental entails diyah, and diyah is compensation for the soul without consideration of equality. Explanation: This is the proof of Imam Abu Hanifa. He says that tadakhul cannot occur, rather it is impossible, because in reality there is a difference between the two acts even if they appear similar, because in the first act qisas for the hand is obligatory, and in the second act qisas for the soul is obligatory. The status of a limb is the status of property, unlike the status of the soul, so how can they be considered one? Because the cause of qisas... both acts entail qisas. In any case, equality in qisas is necessary, and the method of equality is that killing be done in exchange for killing and hand cutting in exchange for hand cutting. It is impossible. And if the situation adopted by the Sahibayn is chosen, i.e., only qisas, then equality is lost because no penalty is given for the hand cutting while equality was necessary. And because... [unclear] is Imam's unique proof. He says that if there is barat (interruption) in between, then by consensus of both parties tadakhul will not occur, rather each act gets its separate punishment. Now consider what barat means: when we reflect, we find that the essence of barat is that due to the interruption, the wound does not progress, its spread stops. When we reflect on the mentioned case, we find that the reality of barat exists even without actual barat, so the same ruling is given as with barat, i.e., no tadakhul. How is that? Because when he intentionally cut the hand, there was a possibility of spread or not, but when he killed, the locus of spread ceased to exist, so the question of the wound spreading is gone, and this non-spread is the essence of barat, so we apply the ruling of barat and say tadakhul will not occur. What is its evidence? Zaid cut Khalid's hand and Bakr then cut off his neck; qisas is obligatory on Bakr, not on Zaid. If Bakr had not killed and the wound caused death, qisas would be on Zaid. So qisas is not on Zaid, only on Bakr. The evidence is that Bakr's killing closed the door of spread, and this non-spread is given the status of barat. Similarly in the mentioned case. Unlike the case where Zaid cut [the hand] and the wound spread until Khalid died, then only qisas is obligatory because it is one act. And unlike the case when Zaid first accidentally cut Khalid's hand and then accidentally killed him before barat, then by consensus tadakhul occurs, and it is the obligation of diyah. Because qatl khata only entails diyah, and diyah is compensation for the soul (the subject), where equality is not considered; equality is only required in qisas. So in the case of qisas, tadakhul does not occur, but in the case of diyah it does. The secret is that in qisas, the penalty is for the act; in diyah, it is for the subject. Since the subject is one, diyah does not multiply, while acts when multiple yield multiple penalties even if the subject is one. So if ten people together accidentally kill one person, one diyah is obligatory because it is qatl khata and it is penalty for the subject, so all ten owe only one diyah. But if ten people together intentionally kill, qisas applies on all because it is penalty for the act, and the acts are multiple. Second proof: Wa lan arsh al-yad innama yajibu 'inda istihkam athar al-fi'l wa dhalika bi al-hazz al-qati' li al-saraya fa-yajtami' daman al-kull wa daman al-juz' fi halatin wahidatin wa la yajtami'an, amma al-qat' wa al-qatl qisasan fa-yajtami'an. Translation: And because the arsh (compensation) for the hand is due only when the effect of the act is established, and that is by the neck-cutting which cuts off the spread, so the guarantee of the whole and the guarantee of the part come together in one state, but they do not normally come together. However, cutting and killing as qisas do come together. Explanation: If both cutting and killing are accidental, why is only diyah sufficient? This is the second proof. He says that if the hand cutting wound does not spread to cause death, the penalty is diyah for the hand, i.e., five thousand dirhams, which is half the full diyah. If it spreads to cause death, full diyah is obligatory. In any case, arsh for the hand is due when the effect of the act is established, i.e., only the hand is cut and no further. In the mentioned case, establishment is known after the neck is cut because the wound no longer has a place to spread. So if you impose diyah for the hand, then you also impose full diyah for the killing, and the full diyah includes the hand's diyah because the whole includes the part. So you have obtained the hand's diyah twice: once separately and once as part of the full diyah. The author refers to this as daman al-kull and daman al-juz'. The two do not normally come together... It is not permissible to collect double compensation for one limb, so we are compelled to say that only one diyah is obligatory and tadakhul occurs. Therefore, in accidental cases we are forced to accept tadakhul. And in intentional cases...
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute (ikhtilaf) between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two leading students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), regarding the issue of tadakhul (merger of penalties) when a person intentionally or accidentally cuts off another's hand and then kills him before the wound from the hand-cutting spreads. The key question: do the penalties for the two acts (qisas for the hand and qisas for the soul) merge into one, or are they both applied separately? The passage begins by explaining the concept of musawat (equality) required in qisas: retaliation must be exactly equal to the injury. Abu Hanifa argues that tadakhul is impossible because the two acts are fundamentally different: the first act (cutting the hand) entails qisas for a limb, which has the status of property, while the second (killing) entails qisas for the soul, which is of a higher order. Equality demands separate retaliation. The Sahibayn apparently hold that only one qisas (for the killing) suffices, but Abu Hanifa responds that this violates equality since the hand injury goes unpunished. He then presents a second argument using the analogy of barat (interruption of causation). If an external interruption (like a third party killing) occurs between the two acts, all schools agree that the penalties are separate. Here, the killing itself acts as an interruption because it prevents the hand wound from spreading, so the same ruling applies: no tadakhul. To illustrate, the example of Zaid and Bakr is given: if Zaid cuts Khalid's hand, then Bakr kills Khalid, qisas is on Bakr only, not Zaid. If Bakr had not killed and the wound killed Khalid, qisas would be on Zaid. The killing by Bakr cuts off the causal chain, so the acts are treated as independent. The same logic applies when one person does both acts. The passage then distinguishes between intentional (amd) and accidental (khata) cases. In accidental cases, tadakhul does occur because the penalty is diyah (blood money), which is compensation for the person (the subject) rather than for the act. Since the subject is one, only one diyah is owed, even if multiple accidental acts occur. The text gives the example of ten people accidentally killing one person: they all owe only one diyah. But if they intentionally kill, each is liable to qisas because the penalty is for the act, and acts are multiple. Finally, a second proof is given specifically for accidental cases: requiring both arsh al-yad (diyah for the hand) and full diyah would result in double compensation for the hand, which is impermissible. Therefore, in accidental cases, the penalties must merge. In intentional cases, the implication is that the penalties do not merge, as per Abu Hanifa's position. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that of Abu Hanifa: no tadakhul in intentional jinayat.

Key Terms
تداخل (tadakhul): The merger or overlapping of penalties such that only one penalty is applied for multiple acts.
مساوات (musawat): Equality between the injury and the retaliation, a necessary condition for qisas.
برات (barat): Interruption of causation that prevents the wound from spreading, making subsequent acts independent.
قصاص (qisas): Retaliation in kind for intentional bodily harm or homicide, requiring equality (musawat).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the barat analogy used by Imam Abu Hanifa, why does the case of Zaid and Bakr (Zaid cuts hand, Bakr kills) support non-merger?
A) Because Zaid and Bakr are two different people, so their acts are automatically separate.
B) Because Bakr's killing interrupts the hand wound's spread, creating an effect equivalent to barat, so the acts are independent.
C) Because Zaid's act is accidental, not intentional.
D) Because Bakr's act happens after the hand cut, so it is a separate crime by default.

Key Takeaway
In intentional bodily harm followed by homicide, Hanafi law holds that penalties for each distinct injury must be applied separately to uphold equality (musawat) in qisas, regardless of whether the acts are by the same person or different persons.

Qisas — Retaliation
advanced
6 min
Integration of Diyah in Manslaughter by Mistake
Explains why diyah for limb and life integrate in khatta but not in 'amad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why diyah integrates in manslaughter by mistake but not in intentional killing, and apply the distinction using the Hanafi proof.

Core Idea
Diyah is compensation for the subject; qisas is for the act. Therefore, diyah integrates, qisas multiplies.

This passage from the Hidayah commentary addresses a key distinction in Hanafi criminal law: when multiple injuries lead to death, does compensation stack or merge? The answer depends on whether the compensation is diyah (for manslaughter by mistake) or qisas (for intentional killing). Let's unpack the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and ruling
The passage begins by stating a case: Zayd mistakenly cuts off Khalid's hand, then before healing, mistakenly kills him. The ruling is that only one diyah is due (tadaakhul). Recognize this as the Hanafi position.
2
Understand the rationale: subject vs. act
The key reason: diyah is compensation for the subject (the victim's life), which is one; qisas is compensation for the act, and multiple acts require multiple compensations. Note that diyah does not consider equality; qisas does.
This is the conceptual foundation.
3
Analyze the second proof (qiyas)
The author provides a second proof: the hand's compensation (arsh) becomes due only when the effect of the act stabilizes. Here, the fatal cut stabilizes both the hand's arsh and the full diyah simultaneously. Since the whole includes the part, collecting both would be double compensation, which is not allowed. Hence integration.
The Arabic phrase 'fa-ijtama'a damān al-kull wa damān al-juz'' captures this.
4
Address the objection about qisas
Why doesn't the same problem arise in 'amad? Because qisas is based on equality and severity. The equality requires that the cutting precede killing; collecting both is permissible as part of the severe punishment.
The author says 'fa-afham wa tadabbar' – understand and reflect.
5
Apply to multiple offenders
The passage extends: if ten people mistakenly kill one, only one diyah is due (subject is one). If they intentionally kill, each is liable for qisas (acts are multiple). This illustrates the rule.

سوال مگر حضرت بعینہ یہی اشکال عمد کی صورت میں بھی واقع ہوتا ہے کیونکہ جب آپ نے اولا قصاص میں ہاتھ کاٹا ہے اور پھر قتل کیا ہے تو چونکہ کل جز کو مشتمل ہوتا ہے۔ لہذا گویا آپنے ہاتھ کا قصاص ڈبل وصول کیا ہے تو اعتراض مذکور یہاں بھی ہے؟ جواب محمد کی سزا میں تغلیظ ہے اور شدت ہے اس لئے یہاں ڈبل وصول جائز ہے۔ کیونکہ قصاص کی بنیاد مساوات پر ہے اور مساوات کا یہی تقاضہ ہے کہ اولا قطع کیا ہو اور پھر قتل کیا جائے اس کو مصنف نے اپنے الفاظ میں ایسے فرمایا ہے۔ اما القطع والقتل قصاصا يجتمعان فافهم و تدبر و تشكر فانه من مزالة الاقدام
Page 94. Book of Homicide. Ashraf al-Bidaya, Urdu commentary of Hidayah, Volume 15. 'And contrary to when they were...' etc. And if Zayd first mistakenly cut off Khalid's hand, and then before healing mistakenly killed him, then in this case there is consensus that integration occurs, and it is the obligation of diyah. Because this is manslaughter by mistake, and in manslaughter by mistake only diyah is obligatory, and diyah is compensation for the life (nafs), i.e., the replacement of the subject, in which equality is not considered; rather equality is obligatory in qisas. Therefore, in the case of qisas, integration will not occur, and in the case of diyah, it will occur. The secret is that in qisas, the recompense is for the act, and in diyah, it is for the subject. And since the subject is one, diyah will not be multiple; and when the acts are multiple, the recompense will also be multiple, even if the whole is one. Hence, if ten people jointly kill one person by mistake, one diyah is obligatory because this is manslaughter by mistake, and since it is recompense for the subject, only one diyah is due upon all ten. And if ten people jointly kill intentionally, qisas applies to all, because it is recompense for the act, and there is multiplicity in the act. Second proof: 'And because the arsh (compensation) of the hand is only obligatory when the effect of the act is established, and that is by the severing cut that prevents transmission, so the guarantee of the whole and the guarantee of the part gather in one state, and they do not gather. As for cutting and killing as qisas, they do gather.' Translation: And because the compensation of the hand is due at the time of the stabilization of the effect of the act, and stabilization is due to the cutting of the neck which prevents transmission, so the guarantee of the whole and the guarantee of the part gather in one state, although these two do not normally gather. Nevertheless, cutting and killing as qisas gather. Explanation: This is the second proof why only diyah suffices when both cutting and killing are by mistake. He says: if the wound from cutting the hand does not transmit to death, then the penalty for cutting the hand is the diyah of the hand, i.e., five thousand dirhams, which is half the full diyah of life. If it transmits to death, then the full diyah is due. In any case, the arsh of the hand is due only when the effect of the act stabilizes, i.e., only up to the hand being cut and not advancing. Here, in the mentioned case, stabilization is known after cutting the neck, because the wound no longer has a path to transmit. So you would make the diyah of the hand obligatory due to cutting the neck, and due to the same cutting of the neck you would also make the full diyah obligatory. And the full diyah includes the diyah of the hand, because the whole includes the part, just as ten includes one. The result is that you have obtained the diyah of the hand twice: first individually, and then combined with the full diyah. This is what the author refers to as the guarantee of the whole and the guarantee of the part. 'And they do not gather'... whereas it is not permissible to collect the diyah of a limb double. Therefore, we are compelled to say that only one diyah is obligatory here, and integration will occur. Hence, in the case of mistake, we are compelled to accept integration. And in the case of intentional, we do not accept integration. Question: But the same objection occurs in the case of intentional, because when you first cut the hand in qisas and then killed, since the whole includes the part, it is as if you have collected qisas for the hand double. So is this objection also present? Answer: In intentional killing, there is severity and intensity in the punishment, so double collection is permissible, because the basis of qisas is equality, and equality requires that the cutting be done first and then killing. The author expressed this in his words: 'As for cutting and killing as qisas, they gather. So understand, reflect, and be grateful, for it is a removal of steps.'
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage explains the Hanafi ruling on integration of compensation when multiple injuries occur in manslaughter by mistake. The author first states the rule: if someone cuts off a person's hand by mistake and then kills that person by mistake before the wound heals, there is consensus that only one diyah is due. The reason is that diyah is compensation for the subject (the victim's life), not for the act. Since the subject is one, multiple diyahs cannot be due. In contrast, qisas is compensation for the act, so if multiple acts are committed, each act requires its own qisas. The author then provides a second proof: the compensation for the hand (arsh) becomes due only when the effect of the act is established, i.e., when the wound stops transmitting. In the case where the neck is cut, the wound from the hand can no longer transmit, so both the hand's arsh and the full diyah would become due at the same time. But since the full diyah includes the hand's diyah, collecting both would result in double compensation for the hand, which is not permitted. Therefore, integration occurs, and only the full diyah is paid. The passage then addresses a potential objection: why does the same issue not arise in qisas? The answer is that qisas is based on equality and severity, so it is permissible to collect multiple compensations. The author concludes with the Arabic sentence 'fa-afham wa tadabbar wa tashakkur fa-innahu min mazallat al-aqdam' (so understand, reflect, and be grateful, for it is a removal of steps).

Key Terms
تداخل (tadaakhul): Integration of multiple compensations into one when the injuries lead to death and the compensation is for the subject.
دية (diyah): Financial compensation paid for manslaughter or injury, varying by the part and severity.
قصاص (qisas): Retaliatory punishment for intentional homicide or injury, based on equality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why does diyah integrate in manslaughter by mistake but not in intentional killing?
A) Because diyah is compensation for the subject, which is one; qisas is compensation for the act, which can be multiple.
B) Because the scholars changed their opinion in the case of intentional killing due to severity.
C) Because the hand injury is too minor to count separately.
D) Because the victim's family has a choice in intentional killing.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when multiple injuries by mistake lead to death, only one diyah is due because compensation is for the subject, not the act.

Qisas — Retaliation

Forgiveness of Cutting and Subsequent Death: Abu Hanifa vs Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on forgiveness scope

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on whether forgiving a cutting (qat) forgives subsequent death, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
Forgiving the cutting alone does not forgive the death unless explicitly stated—Abu Hanifa; Sahibayn: forgiving cutting inherently includes death.

This lesson examines a key intra-Hanafi dispute about the scope of forgiveness when a victim forgives a limb injury but later dies from it. Understanding this difference is crucial for correctly applying rulings on diyat and qisas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the legal issue
The act is a person cutting off another's hand. The legal issue: if the victim forgives the cutting but later dies from it, is the cutter still liable for diyah (blood money)?
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and its conditions
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, forgiveness of the cutting alone does NOT extend to death. The cutter must still pay full diyah. However, if the victim explicitly forgives 'the cutting and what results from it', then death is also forgiven and no diyah is due.
The distinction between confined (muqtasir) and spreading (sari) injury underpins this view.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that forgiving the cutting alone is sufficient to forgive the death as well, because the consequence of cutting is either the cutting itself or death if it spreads.
They argue that 'cutting' linguistically covers both confined and fatal cases.
4
Analyze the Sahibayn's proof
Their proof is that forgiveness of the cutting is forgiveness of its consequence (muijab). The consequence is either qisas for the cutting (if no death) or execution/diyah for murder (if death occurs). So forgiving the cutting forgives whichever consequence occurs. Additionally, the term 'cutting' encompasses both types, so forgiveness covers both.
This is a rational (aqli) argument based on the logic of consequences and linguistic generality.
5
Identify the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Abu Hanifa: if victim forgives only the cutting and later dies, diyah must be paid. Under Sahibayn: no diyah is due. This difference applies also to head wounds (shajjah) that spread and cause death.
The mufta bihi view in the school is generally Abu Hanifa's.

وال اور اب دیت واجب نہ ہوگی۔ البتہ اتنی تفصیل یہاں بھی دیکھنی ہوگی کہ اگر زید نے عمد أخالد کا ہاتھ کا نا تھا اور اس نے دوسرے طریقہ پر معاف کیا ہے تو بس معاف ہو گیا یہ کچھ نہیں دیکھنا ہو گا کہ خالد کتنا مال چھوڑ کر مرا ہے وہ کم ہے یا زیادہ اور اس میں اگر دیت واجب ہوگی تو وہ مابقی کا تہائی ہے یا نہیں؟ اور اگر زید نے خطاء ہاتھ کانٹا ہو اور خالد نے دوسری صورت کے الفاظ سے معاف کیا ہو تو معافی تہائی مال میں جاری ہوگی لہذا مقدار عفو کے علاوہ اگر خالد کا چھوڑا ہوا دو گنا مال ہو تو یہ معافی پوری ثابت ہو جائے گی۔ اور اگر اس کے علاوہ کوئی مال خالد کا نہ ہو تو دیت کی پوری مقدار کا ثلث ۱/۳ معاف ہوگا اور دو ثلث ۲/۳ معاف نہ ہوگا بلکہ اتنی مقدار زید خالد کے ورثا کو دے گا۔

وقالا اذا عفى عن القطع فهو عفو عن النفس ايضا وعلى هذا الخلاف اذا عفاعن الشجة ثم سرى الى النفس ومات

ترجمہ اور صاحبین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ قطع کو معاف کر دینا قطع کے دونوں حکموں کو معاف کر دینا ہے اور اس کا حکم قطع ہے اگر قطع سرایت نہ کرے اور قتل ہے جب کہ سرایت کرے تو ہوگا معاف کرنا قطع سے معاف کرنا تو اس کے دونوں حکموں میں سے ہر ایک سے جو نسا بھی ہو
He said: And whoever cuts off the hand of a man, and the one whose hand is cut off forgives the cutting, then dies from that, then the cutter owes the blood money from his wealth. If he forgives the cutting and what results from it, then dies from that, it is forgiveness of the soul. Then if it was accidental, it is from one-third; if intentional, it is from all wealth. And this is the view of Abu Hanifa. Explanation: Zayd intentionally or accidentally cut off Khalid's hand, and Khalid was a generous man, he forgave the cutting, so it is forgiven; thus neither qisas for the hand nor its diyah is due. But if Khalid died due to that cutting, then the previous forgiveness is cancelled because he only forgave the cutting. This becomes a murder case; therefore diyah is obligatory upon Zayd, whether the cutting was intentional or accidental. However, if Khalid forgave in words like: 'I forgive the cutting and what results from it,' then if he dies, the murder is also forgiven. And then diyah is not due. However, this detail applies: if Zayd intentionally cut Khalid's hand and he forgave in the second manner, then it is simply forgiven without regard to how much wealth Khalid left. And if Zayd accidentally cut the hand and Khalid forgave in the second manner, then the forgiveness applies to one-third of the wealth. So if Khalid left wealth beyond the amount of the forgiving, the full forgiveness is effective; if he left no extra wealth, only one-third of the diyah is forgiven. Anyway, this detail that forgiveness of the cutting does not forgive murder is the madhhab of Imam Abu Hanifa. The Sahibayn differ. So the author says: And the Sahibayn said: When he forgives the cutting, it is forgiveness of the soul as well. And similarly the difference applies when he forgives a head wound (shajjah) then it spreads to the soul and he dies. Explanation: The Sahibayn's madhhab is that when Khalid forgave the cutting, it also forgives the murder. And this difference between Imam Sahib and Sahibayn also applies to head wounds: if Zayd wounds Khalid's head and Khalid forgives the head wound, then Khalid dies, then according to Imam Sahib, the murder is not forgiven, so diyah is due; according to the Sahibayn, the murder is also forgiven. Now the author will present the proofs of both sides. First, the proof of the Sahibayn: Their proof is that forgiveness of the cutting is forgiveness of its consequence; its consequence is either the cutting if it is confined, or murder if it spreads. So forgiveness of the cutting is forgiveness of one of its two consequences, whichever it is. And because the name 'cutting' applies both to the confined and the spreading, so forgiveness of the cutting is forgiveness of its two types. It is like when one forgives the crime, it includes both the confined and the spreading crime; similarly here.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of forgiving a cutting (e.g., of a hand) when the victim subsequently dies from the injury. The matn, attributed to Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, states: If a person cuts off another's hand, and the victim forgives the cutting (without mentioning what results), then later dies from that injury, the cutter must pay the full diyah from his own wealth. However, if the victim forgives both 'the cutting and what results from it' (i.e., explicitly includes any consequences) and then dies, that is a forgiveness of the soul (i.e., the death), and the diyah is waived. For an accidental cutting, forgiveness of the consequences is limited to one-third of the deceased's wealth (like a bequest). For intentional, it applies to the entire wealth (since it's like a pardon before death). This is the position of Imam Abu Hanifa. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differ: they hold that forgiving the cutting alone already constitutes forgiveness of the soul (death) as well, because the cutting inherently includes its possible consequence of death. The text then states that the same disagreement applies to head wounds (shajjah) that spread and cause death. Finally, the passage begins presenting the Sahibayn's proof: The forgiveness of the cutting is forgiveness of its legal consequence. The consequence is either the punishment for the cutting (if no death) or the punishment for murder (if death results). Therefore, forgiving the cutting is forgiving whichever consequence actually occurs. Moreover, the term 'cutting' linguistically encompasses both the confined (non-fatal) and spreading (fatal) injury, so forgiveness covers both types. The Hanafi response to this argument is not included in this window; it presumably follows in subsequent pages. The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, if the victim forgives only the cutting and later dies, the killer still owes diyah. Under Sahibayn, no diyah is due if the cutting was forgiven.

Key Terms
قطع (qat): Cutting off a limb, here specifically a hand, causing a crime.
عفو (afw): Forgiveness, waiving the right to qisas or diyah.
دية (diyah): Blood money, financial compensation for death or loss of limb.
نفس (nafs): The soul or life; 'forgiveness of the soul' means forgiving the death itself.
سارى ومقتصر (sari wa muqtasir): An injury that spreads (sari) leading to death vs. an injury contained (muqtasir) without death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if a victim forgives the cutting of his hand (without mentioning consequences) and later dies from that injury, what is the ruling?
A) The cutter must still pay full diyah.
B) The cutter is completely absolved.
C) The cutter must pay half diyah.
D) The cutter must pay only one-third of diyah.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, forgiveness of a limb injury does not automatically forgive death; explicit wording is required to waive liability for diyah.

Qisas — Retaliation
intermediate
6 min
Effect of Amputation Qisas on Subsequent Death
Intra-Hanafi dispute on effect of limb qisas on death qisas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf regarding the effect of qisas of a limb on subsequent death and apply Imam Muhammad's ruling to a given scenario.

Core Idea
Taking qisas for a limb does not waive the right to qisas for death when victim later dies—intent and knowledge matter.

This passage addresses a nuanced scenario in qisas law: a victim obtains amputation of the perpetrator's hand, but later dies from the same wound. Does the earlier retaliation suffice, or must the perpetrator be executed? The Hanafi imams disagree. Imam Muhammad holds that the death reveals the true crime as murder, and the amputation does not constitute a waiver. Abu Yusuf argues that by choosing amputation, the victim discharged further claims. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to analyze the interplay of intent and knowledge in Islamic criminal law.

Guided Path
1
State Imam Muhammad's ruling
Imam Muhammad holds that the perpetrator must be killed (qisas for death) because the victim's death reveals the original crime was intentional murder. The earlier amputation does not waive the right to qisas for death.
This is the relied-upon view in the text.
2
Identify Abu Yusuf's opposing view
Abu Yusuf holds that the right to qisas for death is dropped because by proceeding with the amputation, the victim impliedly discharged the perpetrator from further liability.
3
Examine the proof of Imam Muhammad
The proof is an analogy (qiyas): a person entitled to qisas for death who takes a limb first does not thereby waive the right to take life later. Similarly, taking the limb here does not waive the right to qisas for death.
The victim’s action was based on a mistaken belief about the extent of his right.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to Abu Yusuf
The text says 'we say': the victim proceeded with the cut thinking his right was only in the cut. He did not knowingly waive the right to qisas for death. Without knowledge, no valid discharge occurs.
Knowledge is essential for an effective waiver ('ibra').
5
Determine the practical consequence
According to Imam Muhammad, the perpetrator is executed. According to Abu Yusuf, the perpetrator is not executed (the amputation suffices). The mufta bihi is Imam Muhammad's view.
This thamarah al-khilaf shows the real-life impact of the dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
چند اصطلاحات ، ایک شخص نے دوسرے کا قصد آہا تھ کا ٹا پھر قاطع سے قصاص لیا گیا اس کے بعد مقطوع زخم کے اثر سے مر گیا تو پہلا قصاص کافی ہے یا نہیں
قال ومن قطعت يده فاقتص له من اليد ثم مات فانه يقتل المقتص منه لانه تبين ان الجناية كانت قتل عمد وحق المقتص له القود واستيفاء القطع لا يوجب سقوط القود كمن له القود اذا استوفى طرف من عليه القصاص وعن ابى يوسف انه يسقط حقه فى القصاص لانه لما اقدم على القطع فقد ابرأه عما وراءه ونحن نقول انما اقدم على القطع ظنا منه ان حقه فيه وبعد السراية تبين انه فى القود فلم يكن مبرنا عنه بدون العلم به.
ترجمہ محمد نے فرمایا اور جس کا ہاتھ کاٹا گیا پس اس کے لئے ہاتھ کا قصاص لے لیا گیا پہ وہ مر گیا تو مقتص منہ کو قتل کیا جائے گا اس لئے کہ (موت کی وجہ سے ) یہ بات واضح ہوگئی کہ جنایت قتل عمد تھی اور منتقص لہ کا حق قصاص تھا اور قطع ید کو وصول کر لینا قصاص کے سقوط کو واجب نہیں کریگا جیسے کہ وہ شخص جس کے لئے قصاص ہو جب کہ وہ وصول کرے من علیہ القصاص کے طرف کو اور ابو یوسف سے منقول ہے کہ اس کا حق قصاص میں ساقط ہو جائے گا اس لئے کہ جب اس (من له القصاص ( قطع پر پیش قدمی کی تو اس نے اس کو ( من عالیہ اختصاص کو ) قطع کے علاوہ سے بری کر دیا اور ہم کہتے ہیں کہ اس نے قطع پر پیش قدمی اس گمان سے کی ہے کہ اس کا حق قطع ہی میں ہے۔
Some terms: A person intentionally cut off another's hand, then the cutter was subjected to qisas (amputation). After that, the amputated person died from the wound. Is the first qisas sufficient or not? He (Muhammad) said: And whoever's hand was cut, then qisas was taken for him from the hand, then he died, then the one from whom qisas was taken is killed because it becomes clear that the crime was intentional murder and the right of the one entitled to qisas is retaliation. Taking the cut does not necessitate dropping the retaliation, like a person entitled to retaliation when he takes a limb from the one on whom retaliation is due. And from Abu Yusuf it is narrated that his right to retaliation is dropped because when he proceeded to cut, he discharged him from what was beyond. And we say: He only proceeded to cut thinking that his right was in the cut, and after the wound spread it became clear that it was in retaliation, so he did not discharge him without knowledge of it.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage discusses a scenario where a person (the victim) has his hand intentionally cut off by another (the perpetrator). The victim then obtains qisas by having the perpetrator's hand cut off. After this amputation, the victim dies from the wound that was initially inflicted. The question is whether the perpetrator must now be killed (qisas for murder) or whether the previously enforced amputation suffices. The commentary presents the view of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani: the perpetrator must be killed because the victim's death reveals that the original crime was actually intentional murder (qatl 'amd), not just injury. The right of the victim (or his heirs) is full retaliation (qisas for death). Taking a limb as qisas does not waive the right to qisas for death, analogous to a case where a person entitled to retaliation takes a limb first—this does not waive his right to later take life if death results. Then it mentions Abu Yusuf's contrary view: when the victim chose to cut the perpetrator's hand, he thereby waived any further claim (since he considered the hand sufficient). The commentary then gives the rebuttal: the victim only proceeded with the cut because he mistakenly believed his right was limited to amputation; he did not knowingly waive the right to qisas for death. Therefore, Imam Muhammad's view prevails: qisas for death is still due.

Key Terms
قصاص (qiṣāṣ): Retaliation; the legal right to inflict equivalent harm on the perpetrator as punishment, either for murder (death) or for bodily injury (limb).
سراية (sarāyah): The spread of a wound or injury causing death; the legal consequence that a non-fatal wound becomes fatal due to subsequent infection or effects.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Muhammad, if a victim obtains qisas of the perpetrator's hand but later dies from the initial wound, what is the ruling?
A) The perpetrator is not killed because qisas has already been taken.
B) The perpetrator must still be killed for murder because the death reveals the true nature of the crime.
C) Only a fine is required for the death.
D) The perpetrator's heirs must pay diyah.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a victim's acceptance of qisas for a limb does not waive the right to qisas for death if the victim later dies from the same wound, because a waiver requires knowledge of the right being waived.

Qisas for Life After Limb Qisas: The Hanafi Position
Ruling on qisas after limb qisas and subsequent death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi majority holds that prior qisas for a limb does not invalidate later qisas for life, and critique Abu Yusuf's counter-argument using the principle of knowledge-based forgiveness.

Core Idea
Qisas for life remains even after limb qisas if death occurs, because forgiveness requires knowledge of the right.

This passage addresses a subtle intra-Hanafi dispute: if a victim's hand is cut in qisas, but the wound later proves fatal, can the offender still be executed for murder? The majority Hanafi position, presented by Imam Muhammad, affirms that the right to life qisas is not lost. We will examine the legal reasoning and the objection of Abu Yusuf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the factual scenario
State the facts: Zayd cuts Bakr's hand intentionally; Zayd's hand is taken in qisas; then Bakr dies from the wound. Identify the legal question.
Focus on the sequence of events and the rights involved.
2
State the Hanafi majority position
Imam Muhammad rules that Zayd must be executed. Why? Because death reveals that the original injury was murder, so the true right was qisas for life (qawd). The limb qisas was based on an incomplete understanding.
The key term: 'تبين' (it became clear).
3
Analyze the proof (qiyas)
The passage draws an analogy: like a guardian who first cuts the murderer's hand, the right to life qisas remains. Identify the asl (guardian's case) and the far' (our case), and the illa (taking a limb does not satisfy the right to life).
This is qiyas jaliy.
4
Present Abu Yusuf's objection
Abu Yusuf argues that accepting the hand qisas implies forgiveness of further consequences. Understand the principle: 'ابرأه عما وراءه' (he absolved him from what is beyond).
This is a legal presumption based on implied consent.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi response and tarjih
The majority responds that forgiveness requires knowledge of the right being forgiven. At the time of limb qisas, the right to life qisas was unknown (because death had not occurred). Therefore, no valid forgiveness occurred. The text sides with Imam Muhammad.
The thamarah al-khilaf: under Abu Yusuf, no execution; under majority, execution.

Muhammad said: And whoever's hand is cut, then qisas is taken from the hand, then he dies — then the one from whom qisas was taken is killed, because it becomes clear that the injury was intentional murder, and the right of the one who took qisas is qisas (life), and taking the cut does not cause the dropping of qisas, like the one who has the right to qisas when he takes a limb from the one upon whom qisas is due. And from Abu Yusuf: that his right to qisas drops because when he proceeded with the cut, he absolved him from what is beyond. And we say: he only proceeded with the cut assuming his right was in it; after the injury spreads it becomes clear that his right was in qisas (life), so he is not an absolver without knowledge of it. Commentary: First understand four terms (mqtas minh, etc.) then the case: Zayd cuts Bakr's hand intentionally, so Zayd's hand is cut in qisas. Then Bakr's wound spreads and he dies. Now Zayd is killed in qisas because it is known that Bakr's right was qisas of life, and cutting the hand does not drop qisas. Example: Zayd kills Bakr intentionally, then the guardian cuts Zayd's hand; qisas is not received nor dropped, so qisas is taken. Imam Abu Yusuf says the right to qisas no longer remains because when Bakr's hand was cut in exchange for Zayd's hand, Bakr absolved Zayd from the remaining consequences. We say: Zayd's hand was cut because the right was to cut the hand, but after spreading it turned out Bakr's right was qisas; absolution cannot occur without knowledge of the right.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the effect of taking qisas for a limb when the victim later dies from the wound. The majority view (Imam Muhammad and the Hanafi school) holds that the initial qisas for the hand does not satisfy the right to qisas for life. Once death occurs, it becomes evident that the crime was murder, and the original right was qisas for life. Analogous to a guardian who cuts the murderer's hand first; the right to life qisas remains. Abu Yusuf argues that by accepting the hand qisas, the victim's representative impliedly forgave further claims. The Hanafi response: the act was done under the mistaken belief that the right was limited to the hand; forgiveness requires knowledge of the full right. Therefore, qisas for life is still applicable.

Key Terms
مقتص منه (muqtass minh): The person from whom qisas is taken (the offender).
مقتص له (muqtass lahu): The person for whom qisas is taken (the victim or representative).
قود (qawd): Qisas for life (retaliation by death).
سراية (sirayah): The spread of an injury leading to death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, prior qisas for a limb does not forfeit the right to qisas for life if the limb wound proves fatal, because the true nature of the crime is only revealed upon death.

Guardian Cuts Murderer's Hand Then Forgives: Liability for Diyah
Ruling on diyah when guardian cuts murderer's hand then forgives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the guardian's liability when he cuts the murderer's hand and then forgives, and explain the reasoning and disagreement with the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Taking a limb without lawful authority requires diyah, even if the taker had a right to the whole life.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in qisas: the guardian of a murder victim cuts the murderer's hand (instead of executing qisas) and then forgives him. Does the guardian owe diyah for the hand? Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) disagree. By understanding their arguments, you will see how the right to qisas does not automatically justify taking a part of the body.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A guardian (wali) of a murder victim cuts the hand of the convicted murderer, then forgives him. The cutting may occur before or after the qisas decree.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and proof
Abu Hanifa holds that the guardian must pay diyat al-yad (half of full diyah). His proof: the guardian's right was to the entire life, not a specific limb; by taking a limb, he exceeded his right, so he is liable.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and proof
Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the guardian owes nothing. Their proof: the guardian took his right—the hand is part of the life he was entitled to destroy—so no liability arises.
4
Compare the proofs
Abu Hanifa focuses on the specificity of the act (taking a limb) exceeding the right to life; Sahibayn view the limb as a fraction of the whole right already possessed.
5
Determine the practical consequence
Under Abu Hanifa, the guardian pays diyat al-yad (e.g., 50 camels); under Sahibayn, no payment is due.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
